  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_71dca5cd-c535-4a6a-907d-2c3da802f73f'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<div id="review_127599075" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/127599075" />
    <a title="Marieke" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1386971-marieke"><img alt="Marieke" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1306621880p2/1386971.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/127599075?book_show_action=true">Oct 23, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marieke" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marieke" href="/user/show/1386971-marieke">Marieke</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385423229" href="/book/show/943984.Adrift_on_the_Nile">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">people interested in Egypt</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=great-african-reads-book-club">great-african-reads-book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=egypt">egypt</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=re-read">re-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1386971-marieke?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer127599075" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15077710531649786057">What?! I didn't write my review yet? Sorry about that, folks. Luckily I scribbled some notes down in a notebook while I read it so I think I might be able pull together a few reflections. This book is a little odd, and not being a Naguib Mahfouz aficionado (yet), I'm not sure how it compares to his other books. It is a slim volume and a fast read. I enjoyed the prose immensely and did not feel like I was reading a translation (someday I will read it in Arabic! Someday, where are you?? Why are yo</span>
  <span id="freeText15077710531649786057" style="display:none">What?! I didn't write my review yet? Sorry about that, folks. Luckily I scribbled some notes down in a notebook while I read it so I think I might be able pull together a few reflections. This book is a little odd, and not being a Naguib Mahfouz aficionado (yet), I'm not sure how it compares to his other books. It is a slim volume and a fast read. I enjoyed the prose immensely and did not feel like I was reading a translation (someday I will read it in Arabic! Someday, where are you?? Why are you so slow in getting here?) but something funny happened to me while I was reading it. After reading the first page or so I said to myself, "Huh? I've <i>seen</i> this movie!" so i stopped to do a Google search as a check against possible insanity. In this case, I was right, there <i>is</i> a film and I <i>have</i> seen it. Oddly, I cannot remember when or where but I <i>think</i> it must have been sometime in 2007 or 2008. Then another funny thing happened as I continued to read. I thought to myself, "I've <i>read</i> this before!" I am sure of it, but unlike in the case of the film, I have absolutely no recollection of when that was.<br /><br />Luckily for me, this book, even though it comes across as somewhat simple, has a lot for the reader to get into and think about. In fact, I gave it four stars because there were aspects that I did not understand at all and I have no way of knowing right now if I would give it five stars if I had understood these things. So after a third or fifth or sixth or fourth reading, I might amend my rating.<br /><br />Clearly I can't count, but that's not important.<br /><br />Anyway, from my philistine vantage, I thought the story was nearly perfect in the way it introduced the characters, built tension, and carried its themes (absurdity versus meaning, first and foremost; how a person functions or doesn't function in a society or political system that is functional or dysfunctional) through to the end. However, the ending itself was odd and confusing to me. I didn't feel like I had read a proper ending. I turned the page looking for more text but didn't find any. So I returned to the previous page and read it again. Same feeling. This time I turned the page and checked the binding to see if the last page had been torn out. Nope. Another reason for four stars instead of five, actually; although I am not sure if the problem is with me or the book. Sayyid Mahfouz, what was your intent??<br /><br />I know the bare basics of Egypt's modern history, but reading this book returned a feeling to me that I had felt when I visited Egypt in the winter of 2007--I want to learn more about <i>all</i> of Egypt's history. Ancient history is not boring! Nor is the history between ancient and now (or rather, Nasser's period, since that is the setting of the book and also when it was written). Anis, the protagonist, has fairly consistent hallucinations (I <i>think</i> they are hallucinations) throughout the story about historical figures and events--he receives a number of visits from Jonah's whale, for instance, emerging from the Nile--and although I'm familiar with each of them, I do not know their significance to an Egyptian person (or if they are even at all significant to an Egyptian person). Without that, I confess to feeling a little bit lost in that layer of the story.<br /><br />Perhaps someday Anis's hallucinations will make sense to me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15077710531649786057" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2315749382" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2315749382" />
    <a title="Lauren " class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1291915-lauren"><img alt="Lauren " src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604235645p2/1291915.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2315749382?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lauren " class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lauren " href="/user/show/1291915-lauren">Lauren </a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423335" href="/book/show/5498.Adrift_on_the_Nile">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=1960s-fiction">1960s-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1291915-lauren?shelf=translated-works">translated-works</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2315749382" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer679394066668003601">This glimpse of intellectual Egyptian life in the 1960s was reminiscent of Albert Camus, and a stark contrast to the way Egypt is depicted in literature and media today.<br/><br/>A group of friends meet weekly on a Nile houseboat to smoke hookah and discuss philosophy: lots of societal obligations, gender roles, absurdity, and nihilism. It is staged and written like a play, and that style works very well throughout the short book (my copy was just over 160 pages). I enjoyed the peek into life in this time</span>
  <span id="freeText679394066668003601" style="display:none">This glimpse of intellectual Egyptian life in the 1960s was reminiscent of Albert Camus, and a stark contrast to the way Egypt is depicted in literature and media today.<br /><br />A group of friends meet weekly on a Nile houseboat to smoke hookah and discuss philosophy: lots of societal obligations, gender roles, absurdity, and nihilism. It is staged and written like a play, and that style works very well throughout the short book (my copy was just over 160 pages). I enjoyed the peek into life in this time, and will definitely return to Mahfouz, a Nobel prize winner and bastion of modern Arabic literature.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="679394066668003601" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Jenny" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6565711-jenny"><img alt="Jenny" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1324233459p2/6565711.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/452838955?book_show_action=true">Nov 10, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jenny" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jenny" href="/user/show/6565711-jenny">Jenny</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9783518223826" href="/book/show/6596660-das-hausboot-am-nil">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6565711-jenny?shelf=2014">2014</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6565711-jenny?shelf=philosophy">philosophy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6565711-jenny?shelf=nobelprize">nobelprize</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6565711-jenny?shelf=persian-arabic">persian-arabic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer452838955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10424688065236752089">Anis Zaki is what one might call an escapist. It is the 1960's he and his friends gather every evening in order to smoke <i>kif</i> on his houseboat, escaping from the reality of life in Nasser's Egypt. In a state of a more or less constant high, they discuss, philosophize and halluzinate together, creating their own absurd paradise where the rules of reality no longer apply:<br/><br/><i> "Love is an old and worn-out game, but it is sport on the houseboat. Fornication is held as a vice by councils and institutions,</i></span>
  <span id="freeText10424688065236752089" style="display:none">Anis Zaki is what one might call an escapist. It is the 1960's he and his friends gather every evening in order to smoke <i>kif</i> on his houseboat, escaping from the reality of life in Nasser's Egypt. In a state of a more or less constant high, they discuss, philosophize and halluzinate together, creating their own absurd paradise where the rules of reality no longer apply:<br /><br /><i> "Love is an old and worn-out game, but it is sport on the houseboat. Fornication is held as a vice by councils and institutions, but it is freedom on our houseboat. Women are all conventions and marriage deeds in the home, but they are nubile and alluring on the houseboat. And the moon is a satellite, dead and cold, but on the houseboat, it is poetry ..."</i> <br /><br />As Samara, a journalist and aspiring playwright enters this kif-fueled enclave, tensions and erotic complications slowly erode the fragile harmony of that escapist refuge, and paradise is transformed into hell as reality seeps in.<br /><br />Interestingly, this novel for large parts reads like a chamber play. Line upon line of mere dialogue, some hard to follow without sufficient background knowledge of politics and life in Egypt at the time, some mesmerizing and poetic.<br />With one foot deep in existentialism it elegantly asks about the design of human nature, about what to do with ourselves when nothing seems worth doing.<br /><br />I am sure this won't be my last Mahfouz.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10424688065236752089" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_81443930" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/81443930" />
    <a title="Leon" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1477194-leon"><img alt="Leon" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1503495500p2/1477194.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/81443930?book_show_action=true">Dec 18, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leon" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leon" href="/user/show/1477194-leon">Leon</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423335" href="/book/show/5498.Adrift_on_the_Nile">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer81443930" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2457354074443636032">This book is my first venture or introduction to writings from the middle east, specifically Egypt. So I approached it with some prejudice, inevitably because so far I’ve only read fiction from the West and a couple from the East, most from Japan.<br/><br/>After successfully reading past the first chapter, I was surprised to actually begin to like this book. The absurdist element Naguib Mahfouz introduced very early on in the story sold me entirely, lock, stock and barrel. I was amused by Anis, who works </span>
  <span id="freeText2457354074443636032" style="display:none">This book is my first venture or introduction to writings from the middle east, specifically Egypt. So I approached it with some prejudice, inevitably because so far I’ve only read fiction from the West and a couple from the East, most from Japan.<br /><br />After successfully reading past the first chapter, I was surprised to actually begin to like this book. The absurdist element Naguib Mahfouz introduced very early on in the story sold me entirely, lock, stock and barrel. I was amused by Anis, who works in some government department. Given the task of annotating some procedures, he never realizes at all that his pen had no ink when he was writing, so that he presents pages of blank papers to his superior, who then, rightly, accuses him of being a drug addict.<br /><br />Which he is, in some way. Every day after work, come evening he takes to a riverboat on the Nile, to smoke hashish with a group of friends. There some old man prepares a water pipe for them. They pass the pipe around and get high, and talk about love and life. <br /><br />Most of the action in the book centres around the riverboat, and the dialogue actually sounds like a transcription from a play. Which is incidentally what a new guest, a female journalist, Samara, is writing. We know this because Anis looked into Samara’s notebook, and found that she has taken as the players his friends in the riverboat.<br /><br />With pages upon pages of mere dialogue in this vein, you might be tempted to abandon the book halfway. But, believe me, you won’t, when the talk among Anis’s friends are flirtatious, about women and sex, and philosophical, about the meaning of life then in the 60s of Gamal Abdel Nasser, their country’s leader.<br /><br />Things stop being idyllic when they decide to drive out in the middle of the night, all of them drunk and high. Returning, their car hits something, they are not sure what. But, the next day they found out from the news, that they killed a man. Guilt follows, especially Anis’ and Samara’s. Anis breaks down from the stress and gets fired from his job. He tries to persuade the others to report their involvement to the police, but they refuse. One of their arguments is that the man’s death is one among many that is a commonality in Egypt.<br /><br />Adrift on the Nile was made into an Egyptian movie in 1971. It is in black and white, which makes me nostalgic for those 60s black and white Eqyptian comical movies Malaysian TV used to show years back. Today you can still sometimes catch such movies on Astro’s Eqyptian channel.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2457354074443636032" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_162639763" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/162639763" />
    <a title="Nick" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/622520-nick"><img alt="Nick" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1507662819p2/622520.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/162639763?book_show_action=true">Apr 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nick" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nick" href="/user/show/622520-nick">Nick</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780385423229" href="/book/show/943984.Adrift_on_the_Nile">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/622520-nick?shelf=africa">africa</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer162639763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1174110108690992517">Egypt in the 1960's -- Cairo and it's climate of intelligentsia -- brings us this existentialist story that skirts the borders of feminism and anti-establishment. The thesis in this virtually plotless work is that men have the luxury of being absurdist heroes in their own lives, but women, because of the seriousness of their position as human nurturers, do not. Thus the men in the story are all absurd, that is they recognize the absurdity of existence. The women who try to do the same are brande</span>
  <span id="freeText1174110108690992517" style="display:none">Egypt in the 1960's -- Cairo and it's climate of intelligentsia -- brings us this existentialist story that skirts the borders of feminism and anti-establishment. The thesis in this virtually plotless work is that men have the luxury of being absurdist heroes in their own lives, but women, because of the seriousness of their position as human nurturers, do not. Thus the men in the story are all absurd, that is they recognize the absurdity of existence. The women who try to do the same are branded as frivolous and flighty, but even they, by the end, realize the advantages of rolling their eyes wheere the men are concerned. For it is the men, in their absurdist state, who end up contemplating their own frivolity. <br /><br />Mafhour's book reminded me a lot of French New Wave cinema, which might be why "Adrift on the Nile" became a popular art-house movie in the West in the 1970's, despite it's Arabic language. It's sort of like Godard/Truffaut's "Breathless" -- the action, such as it is, springs up at you in flurries, then dissipates into long stretches of contemplation.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1174110108690992517" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3257135?book_show_action=true">Jul 19, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sean Sullivan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sean Sullivan" href="/user/show/75637-sean-sullivan">Sean Sullivan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3257135" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17153780137127946293">I was told to read this because it was supposed to be “the perfect treatment of life under Nasser”. It’s the story of a group of pseudo intellectuals and broken men who meet on a house boat every night to get high, until a tragic event befalls them leading at least one of them to kinda sorta reexamine his life. I guess it is supposed to be an indictment of educated classes, wasting away their lives while Rome burned, but I don’t know. I’m not really very good at this allegorical stuff.<br/><br/>Anyway, at</span>
  <span id="freeText17153780137127946293" style="display:none">I was told to read this because it was supposed to be “the perfect treatment of life under Nasser”. It’s the story of a group of pseudo intellectuals and broken men who meet on a house boat every night to get high, until a tragic event befalls them leading at least one of them to kinda sorta reexamine his life. I guess it is supposed to be an indictment of educated classes, wasting away their lives while Rome burned, but I don’t know. I’m not really very good at this allegorical stuff.<br /><br />Anyway, at least in the translation I read, it isn’t a very good book. I can’t tell whether this is because the translator failed to capture Mahfouz’s usual dead-on character development or if Mahfouz just isn’t as capable of capturing drug haze middle class malise. Either way, if you’re dipping into Mahfouz, skip this one<br /></span>

<div id="review_410909799" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/410909799" />
    <a title="Lamski Kikita" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1439240-lamski-kikita"><img alt="Lamski Kikita" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1321835207p2/1439240.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/410909799?book_show_action=true">Sep 09, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lamski Kikita" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lamski Kikita" href="/user/show/1439240-lamski-kikita">Lamski Kikita</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer410909799" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15140365675539631418">I was very disappointed, being a huge fan of Mahfouz' work. This book seemed like a random rant/ rambling. A story of a group of people who get together and get high, and philosophize. But to me, despite some good lines and several interesting turns in the story, it seemed like good old Naguib got high himself and rambled on and on. I would not recommend this book, and I can't wait to start reading some of his better stuff.</span>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_959328728" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/959328728" />
    <a title="Amandine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8700095-amandine"><img alt="Amandine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334074216p2/8700095.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/959328728?book_show_action=true">Jun 06, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amandine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amandine" href="/user/show/8700095-amandine">Amandine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423335" href="/book/show/5498.Adrift_on_the_Nile">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer959328728" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14596858183344355067">For the entire duration of this book, I wished I had the original Arabic version or that I could see the movie. I speak a little Arabic, enough that I could probably make sense of story if I had a dictionary on hand and a lot of patience. Reading it was similar to how I've experienced the few plays that I've read - I had the sense that the bare bones of the characterization was there, that there are some compelling things going on, but I needed something else to make the characters really seem l</span>
  <span id="freeText14596858183344355067" style="display:none">For the entire duration of this book, I wished I had the original Arabic version or that I could see the movie. I speak a little Arabic, enough that I could probably make sense of story if I had a dictionary on hand and a lot of patience. Reading it was similar to how I've experienced the few plays that I've read - I had the sense that the bare bones of the characterization was there, that there are some compelling things going on, but I needed something else to make the characters really seem like individuals and to feel the full tension in their interactions. Maybe what I was missing was the increased poetry of the original prose, or maybe it was just actors who could show distinct mannerisms and emote. <br /><br />The story is based on an ensemble cast of middle to upper class Egyptians in the Nasser era. They gather to smoke kif (which I think is hashish) on a houseboat on the Nile after work. It was a fascinating snapshot of the moral landscape of the time and place. The pinnacle of unassailable morality is faithful marriage and religious devotion, but those who deviate from it seem pretty sure in their choices, and in this story anyway are the majority. The servant Amm Abdou is the symbol of this contradiction. He both leads morning prayer and procures prostitutes and drugs for the boat (but does not partake in either). He is also essentially treated as sub-human by the rest of the boat. He works constantly and doesn't even have a blanket to sleep under. <br /><br />The narrator, meanwhile, is Anis, a disaffected, moody civil servant who runs the boat, setting up the smoking equipment but rarely speaking. His narrative frequently lapses into semi-reality, and his literal pipe dreams become intermixed with actual events. I liked this aspect of the book, it really is reality for Anis. <br />In particular he searches for some kind of a whale - a reference to Moby-Dick, but I was never sure if Anis was intentionally using the reference or if it was Mahfouz drawing the comparison for us. <br /><br />No one on the boat really seems to like each other, but they fall in love. To me, it is a impractical, delusional kind of love that seems to be mostly lust. One particularly handsome movie actor, Ragab I think it is, runs through a lot of women. When the otherwise respectable and well-organized journalist Samara joins the boat, it turns out to research a play, he somehow becomes entangled with her. This is unfortunate for Anis, a widower, who is also in love with her. To be honest I found their involvement, vague though it was, annoying. Other than beauty and success, I didn’t see why Ragab held much power over Samara, who seemed to have a head on her shoulders.<br /><br />Anis, meanwhile, really doesn’t. He has been fucking up at work, letting hashish and melancholy rules his life and getting more bitter toward his boss the Director General. At the end of the book, someone has the suspect idea to all pile into a car and go to the country - against Anis’s wishes. Ragab, seeking thrill, goes much too fast on the way back and hits and kills a man. In the daze of the accident, Anis loses his job. <br /><br />This part of the book I found very gripping. They originally more or less decide as a group not to do anything and keep driving. Then there is a confrontation between the parties the next day. The basic issue at hand is whether they, specifically Ragab, should turn themselves in - it is what is expected from just society, but they would not be able to revive the dead man and would only ruin their lives. But yet none of them are left alone by their consciousnesses. Anis and Ragab fight, Anis nagging him to surrender essentially because he has nothing to lose. The book concludes with his confession to Samara of his love, and then just ends, many lives ruined, and Anis without any kind of plan. And I have to say, I love endings that do not conclude the story but demonstrate what you have learned about the characters and the inconclusiveness of life. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14596858183344355067" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3223911279" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3223911279" />
    <a title="Marwan Emad" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52733642-marwan-emad"><img alt="Marwan Emad" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528139666p2/52733642.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3223911279?book_show_action=true">Mar 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Marwan Emad" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Marwan Emad" href="/user/show/52733642-marwan-emad">Marwan Emad</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3223911279" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5539708566687405691">4.5<br/>This is one of the most rich novels I have read for Naguib Mahfouz, and as always, you can look at this novel from more than a single point of view. <br/>One of them is, we see those whole believe in 'Purpose' VS those who believe in 'Nothing'. We are being given a strong debate between both believers in more than a single dialogue, and we see how easily one can jump from believing in one to the other. The way Naguib depicted the mindset of each is just unique. No words can give it its due credit<br/>A</span>
  <span id="freeText5539708566687405691" style="display:none">4.5<br />This is one of the most rich novels I have read for Naguib Mahfouz, and as always, you can look at this novel from more than a single point of view. <br />One of them is, we see those whole believe in 'Purpose' VS those who believe in 'Nothing'. We are being given a strong debate between both believers in more than a single dialogue, and we see how easily one can jump from believing in one to the other. The way Naguib depicted the mindset of each is just unique. No words can give it its due credit<br />Another one is, Naguib's usual, our Egyptian community. He gives a representative from each faction of our community, and again, shows who they truly are. Naguib keeps on insisting that we , Egyptians, tend to take care of "having a job" regardless of doing this job in the way we should be or not. That we tend to take care of "how moral our thoughts should be" regardless of how moral our acts are. He keeps on showing us how we are never truthful to ourselves, and that we're all sinners in one way or the other.<br />Another version of this story is, maybe, depicting the era at which it was written, an era when all people believed themselves to be doing a well respected noble job, but in the end, it was all just a puff of smoke in the air.<br />My favorite character is definitely Anis. He was, as my friend put it, always true to himself. He knew he could indulge in all the pleasure of life for as much as he wants, as long as he is not hurting any other. We see how he rebels against his belief in "Nothingness" and how he defied Samara's weak moral beliefs (I won't say fake beliefs though), but whether out of his "yearn for purpose despite his nothingness" or out of his desire to uncover Samar's true identity, that we will never know<br /><br />All in all, this was a very deep Novel that deserves to be read again and again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5539708566687405691" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2060011115" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2060011115" />
    <a title="Vel Veeter" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/69444656-vel-veeter"><img alt="Vel Veeter" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588168332p2/69444656.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2060011115?book_show_action=true">Jul 14, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Vel Veeter" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vel Veeter" href="/user/show/69444656-vel-veeter">Vel Veeter</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780385423335" href="/book/show/5498.Adrift_on_the_Nile">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2060011115" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3275773923212524965">It’s too easy to conflate one novel of an author with the total of that author’s work, as it’s also too easy to conflate an author winning the Nobel Prize as being a representative of their respective country as a stand-in for all literature of that country. Obviously there’s some Western chauvinism at play in doing so but also it’s quite limiting in its estimation of what that body of work might contain.<br/><br/>Adrift on the Nile is a very different novel from Palace Walk (and by extension the other no</span>
  <span id="freeText3275773923212524965" style="display:none">It’s too easy to conflate one novel of an author with the total of that author’s work, as it’s also too easy to conflate an author winning the Nobel Prize as being a representative of their respective country as a stand-in for all literature of that country. Obviously there’s some Western chauvinism at play in doing so but also it’s quite limiting in its estimation of what that body of work might contain.<br /><br />Adrift on the Nile is a very different novel from Palace Walk (and by extension the other novels in the Cairo Trilogy if they maintain the same basic structures and style and subject of the first one). This novel is only 150 pages or so, but still represents a full picture of a time, a place, and a sensibility. Still in Cairo, but representing some 50 years later, this novel tells the story of a group of cosmopolitan friends who drink, discuss art, and think through their situation in the aftermath of a regimes in Egypt that no longer rewards a casual intellectual class with the life of leisure they so desire. It’s not the same feel as a dystopian novel where the destruction of democracy has led to a rounding up of dissidents, but certainly could feel that way if your way of life were in danger. It turns out that in the fallout of empire the labor of the country still actually had to be done by someone, and so now that people are being called to account for their role in new country, those who contributed in much more abstract (or not at all) ways find it difficult to make their way.<br /><br />This is an interesting, if slight, novel. It doesn’t have the ooomph of the previous by Mahfouz, but it has an impact equally in proportion to its goal and subject.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3275773923212524965" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/153411-dhei?shelf=international-lit">international-lit</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2746800664" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16314816837289044290">I had no expectation when I was about to read this story. The beginning was interesting, but I started to doubt when almost half of book had 'random talks' with no plot happened. I might not really understand what philosophy they talked about. They definitely talked about existensialism. But 1/4 near end of the book, the tension rose. And everything started to pace faster and faster. <br/><br/>From the beginning, it was hard to tell where the story would bring. I enjoyed how the main character started to </span>
  <span id="freeText16314816837289044290" style="display:none">I had no expectation when I was about to read this story. The beginning was interesting, but I started to doubt when almost half of book had 'random talks' with no plot happened. I might not really understand what philosophy they talked about. They definitely talked about existensialism. But 1/4 near end of the book, the tension rose. And everything started to pace faster and faster. <br /><br />From the beginning, it was hard to tell where the story would bring. I enjoyed how the main character started to stone and dreaming. The narration changed from 3rd POV to 1st POV and returned to 3rd without any 'idling' line. <br /><br />For people who enjoy novel, this story should be considered literary genre. For aspiring novelist, they should read this book. Mainly to learn how to write a smooth, an unexpected change from 3rd POV to 1st one. And how a story that seemed had no plot, indeed had a plot!<br /><br />Reading some reviews here, I wasn't surprised that this book had made its way into movie. When reading, I could picture it as a film. <br /><br />Of all the smooth knots between plots, characters, and themes, now I understand why Mahfouz is one of the best writer.</span>

The reader is caught between the bohemian group’s nihilistic and absurd philosophy and Samara’s realism and belief in the Absolute, free love no constraints vs. marriage, staying sober in a mundane job vs. the nightlife of kif and hookah that make you talk to pharaohs, Mamluk kings and prophets.   <span id="freeText9529428080578717035" style="display:none">Not his best, but still good. The reader is caught between the bohemian group’s nihilistic and absurd philosophy and Samara’s realism and belief in the Absolute, free love no constraints vs. marriage, staying sober in a mundane job vs. the nightlife of kif and hookah that make you talk to pharaohs, Mamluk kings and prophets. Take your pick. <br />Subtle hints are scattered here and there that criticize Nasser’s Egypt. The Pyramids scene is near the murder scene where they hit a peasant (symbolic of Egypt) and run. The controversial minor character, Amm Abduh, brings hookers and kif to the houseboat yet calls for prayers in the mosque nearby.<br />This is an unusual Egyptian novel written in the sixties. The film made in 1971 adds more depth, details and characters. </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8614856984081692670">Should be read as a companion piece to Camus' 'The Fall.' Divides the world's attitudes into a dichotomy of 'Seriousness'-which ignores the inherent meaningless of reality, and 'Absurd'- which is truer but unsatisfying. Finds some solace in opiates, women, and friends, but ultimately implies existence is a solitary quest (and possibly unanswerable). </span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer3405464027" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7781757525233560326">This novel features a group of friends who regularly meet in the evening on a houseboat on the River Nile to talk about the issues of the day but mainly to smoke kif through a water-pipe. <br/><br/>The viewpoint starts off as that of Anis Zaki, a civil servant with troubles at work and whose wife and daughter died many years previously. Anis’s mind can wander and he has occasional illusions – of the Caliph Harun-al-Rashid, of a whale in the Nile, of conversing with the pharaoh Thutmose III. Sometimes, how</span>
  <span id="freeText7781757525233560326" style="display:none">This novel features a group of friends who regularly meet in the evening on a houseboat on the River Nile to talk about the issues of the day but mainly to smoke kif through a water-pipe. <br /><br />The viewpoint starts off as that of Anis Zaki, a civil servant with troubles at work and whose wife and daughter died many years previously. Anis’s mind can wander and he has occasional illusions – of the Caliph Harun-al-Rashid, of a whale in the Nile, of conversing with the pharaoh Thutmose III. Sometimes, however, the narrative focus shifts to something more objective.<br /><br />Others of the company are Ahmad Nasr, notoriously faithful to his wife; Mustafa Rashid, a well-known lawyer; Ali al-Sayid, a famous art critic; Khalid Azzuz, a writer; Ragab al-Qadi, the group’s womaniser in chief. Women are not excluded; Layla Zaydan, a translator, is introduced to new members as “beautiful and cultured” not least in that her golden hair is real, not a wig, while Saniya Kamil turns up whenever her husband has committed an indiscretion. The houseboat is looked after by general factotum Amm Abduh, huge in stature, who mostly keeps himself to himself but when summoned will refresh the water-pipe. As well as making the call-to-prayer at the local mosque he will procure street girls for the members. The group’s female members, despite occasionally spending the night in rooms on the boat, are contrasted to the street girls in that, “‘they are respectable ladies,’” the rationale being, “‘They don’t sell themselves. They give and take, just like men.’”<br /><br />The text is mostly dialogue, there is not much of a plot here. There is some disquiet one evening when Ragab appears with the teenage Sana al-Rashidi, a student; even more when journalist Samara Barghat arrives, the object of suspicion due to her calling (possibly not unjustified suspicion, revealed when Anis takes the opportunity to rummage in her handbag one evening and filches a notebook which contains a scenario and characters for a play – all based on the houseboat’s habituees.) The only incident occurs on a car journey out of the city, to which Anis had only reluctantly acceded, when, travelling too fast on their return, they hit a pedestrian. But all agree to keep quiet about it.<br /><br />By showing us a slice of middle-class Egyptian life in the 1960s (when the book was first published in Arabic,) <em>Adrift on the Nile</em> reveals the uneasy connection between Egypt’s past and its then present by subtle indirection. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7781757525233560326" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer8309727937731121700">1966, so about ten years after Cairo Trilogy.<br/><br/>This is an older Mahfouz, perhaps cynical is the word, sketching half a dozen middle-class Cairo residents getting together evenings to smoke pot [kif], all of them feeling it is useless to be involved at all in politics or trying to change society. Quite hard for me to make sense of what they say; they mean their conversation to remain light-hearted, and therefore and making jokes or cynical remarks that are hard to place [given I totally don't know </span>
  <span id="freeText8309727937731121700" style="display:none">1966, so about ten years after Cairo Trilogy.<br /><br />This is an older Mahfouz, perhaps cynical is the word, sketching half a dozen middle-class Cairo residents getting together evenings to smoke pot [kif], all of them feeling it is useless to be involved at all in politics or trying to change society. Quite hard for me to make sense of what they say; they mean their conversation to remain light-hearted, and therefore and making jokes or cynical remarks that are hard to place [given I totally don't know the situation or culture they were in].<br /><br />Transl. 1993, so it seems only after he won the Nobel Prize [1988] did the English world decide to publish his works.<br /><br />Wikipedia:<br /><br />"Mahfouz remained a bachelor until age 43 because he believed that, with its numerous restrictions and limitations, marriage would hamper his literary future "I was afraid of marriage . . . especially when I saw how busy my brothers and sisters were with social events because of it. This one went to visit people, that one invited people. I had the impression that married life would take up all my time. I saw myself drowning in visits and parties. No freedom."<br /><br />However, in 1954, he quietly married a Coptic Orthodox woman from Alexandria, Atiyyatallah Ibrahim, with whom he had two daughters, Fatima and Umm Kalthum. The couple initially lived on *****a houseboat in the Agouza section of Cairo on the west bank of the Nile*****, then moved to an apartment along the river in the same area.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer16170725609655886625">Mahfouz tells a great story. This one's spun around a pretty simple plot, about a group of professionals who embrace their feeling of irrelevance to history quite cheerfully, until history intervenes and makes clear their implication in it. A kind of resolution of their meaning dialogue, which has been loosely structured around an argument between seriousness of purpose (brought to bear by a woman outsider, who troubles the group by not quite fitting their expectations) and acknowledging, resign</span>
  <span id="freeText16170725609655886625" style="display:none">Mahfouz tells a great story. This one's spun around a pretty simple plot, about a group of professionals who embrace their feeling of irrelevance to history quite cheerfully, until history intervenes and makes clear their implication in it. A kind of resolution of their meaning dialogue, which has been loosely structured around an argument between seriousness of purpose (brought to bear by a woman outsider, who troubles the group by not quite fitting their expectations) and acknowledging, resignedly, the absurd. For most of them, everything appears to be purely fodder for abstract debate, until events disprove it... thus.<br /><br />It opens with the central "master of ceremonies," the civil servant Anis, getting in trouble because he hasn't noticed that the pen he used to write a report for his boss ran out of ink soon after he started. It's a terrific metaphor for a more general failure to make a mark.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16170725609655886625" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4245048183623347161">I read the first 30 pages of the book and I still was not sure where the book was coming from or going. Samara enters this "absurdist" paradise creating erotic tensions in the house boat and a bit of drama. <br/><br/>Naguib wrote beautifully an</span>
  <span id="freeText4245048183623347161" style="display:none">I read the first 30 pages of the book and I still was not sure where the book was coming from or going. The plot was not easy to decipher, until I just allowed myself to drift along the Nile with the characters. Quite a simple plot indeed of friends who meet every evening at Anis Zaki's house boat to escape their lives in Nasser's Egypt smoking either Kif or Hashish. Samara enters this "absurdist" paradise creating erotic tensions in the house boat and a bit of drama. <br /><br />Naguib wrote beautifully and through Anis' Kif fuelled fantasies he is able to introduce some philosophy and some poetry. Its a good read as long as one does not stop reading after 30 or so pages. The book is short and nice.</span>

  <span id="freeText5248510657441384630" style="display:none">A group of people from the old bourgeoisie meet on a houseboat on the Nile every week to smoke cannabis and talk about life and art. The book reads like a gradual drifting into a dreamlike state - like going from alertness to intoxication as the individuals did each time they met. Until one night the dream turns to a nightmare and paradise is lost.<br />This is my third Mahfouz story and I'm glad I did not read it first. I enjoyed Respected Sir and Miramar more but I will continue to read Mahfouz as each of his stories is unique and I get to know different kinds of people in each book.</span>

<div id="review_3327237509" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3327237509" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer287398243742829190">It was my first book by Naguib Mahfous and although I can't praise enough the beautiful descriptions and metaphors, the main point of the book was not clear and it was difficult to focus. Though what else can it be like when you are reading about nihilists? </span>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('jHmjUYVDJCVw7XBIH4coXefWgQKj+baQDoEqSghB9YWrcbz3N9fEtN4tW5wUEEjHze9POH9XJEOkFIigAgBA2Q==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('mDTOKjA/dCSNOUb+QUk0gRanwt68lJDVHwLD75k3Tk6/PNGMgquUtSP5bSpK3lQbPJ4M5GA6Aga1l2EFk3b7Eg==')})
  }
//]]>
</script>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer6648232750118917219">Naguib Mahfouz (Arabic author profile: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4273240._' rel='nofollow noopener'>نجيب محفوظ</a>) was an Egyptian writer who won the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature. He published over 50 novels, over 350 short stories, dozens of movie scripts, and five plays over a 70-year career. Many of his works have been made into Egyptian and foreign films.</span>
  <span id="freeText6648232750118917219" style="display:none">Naguib Mahfouz (Arabic author profile: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4273240._" rel="nofollow noopener">نجيب محفوظ</a>) was an Egyptian writer who won the 1988 Nobel Prize for Literature. He published over 50 novels, over 350 short stories, dozens of movie scripts, and five plays over a 70-year career. Many of his works have been made into Egyptian and foreign films.</span>
